The Court does not prohibit the use of AI, but requires compliance with Rule 11 of the Federal Rules of Civil Procedure and other applicable legal and ethical standards.\n\n### Recent Alabama AI Cases\n\nThere have been significant AI-related sanctions in Alabama federal courts:\n\n- In July 2025, a federal judge in Alabama imposed sanctions on three attorneys for submitting court filings containing fabricated legal citations generated by ChatGPT, with the false citations appearing in two motions filed on behalf of a defendant in an Alabama Department of Corrections case.\n\n- Attorneys Matthew B. Reeves, William J. Cranford and William R. Lunsford were disqualified from further participation in the case and referred to the Alabama State Bar for disciplinary proceedings, with U.S. District Judge Anna M. Manasco writing that \"Fabricating legal authority is serious misconduct that demands a serious sanction\".\n\n### Broader Context\n\nIn 2025, 38 states adopted or enacted around 100 AI-related measures, and this trend continues into 2026. In 2025 alone, federal courts in Alabama, Colorado, New Jersey, Texas, and Wyoming, among others, imposed sanctions on lawyers who submitted filings containing fictitious case citations generated by AI.\n\n**Note:** Individual judges in the Middle District may have their own chambers practices or orders regarding AI use that are not publicly posted online. Kelly Jr. acknowledged there was nothing \"inherently problematic\" with using AI, so long as an attorney does not attempt to persuade a court with fake cases.\n\n### Federal Landscape\n\nSince Judge Brantley Starr of the US District Court for the Northern District of Texas issued the first standing order on the use of AI in preparing court filings in 2023, over 200 state and federal judges have amended or issued standing orders, general orders, local rules, pretrial orders, and other guidance to address AI use and misuse in their courtrooms.\n\n### Work Product and Privilege Issues\n\nA Magistrate Judge in the Colorado district court noted that \"AI is forcing litigants and courts to confront difficult questions\" about confidentiality and work product, and in two recent cases, US district courts in Colorado and Michigan held that a self-represented party was entitled to claim privilege over their interactions with a public AI tool.\n\nThe search results indicate that the District of Colorado has been actively addressing AI use through individual judge standing orders rather than district-wide local rules as of 2024-2026."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"338a3d32-ceef-43a1-99be-6eacec6ac740","uuid":"8887ce24-2d6a-4642-8145-6ef6f10c3e3f"}
